      B REAKING THE C URSE OF M ULTIAGENCY IN ROBUST
002   M ULTI -AGENT R EINFORCEMENT L EARNING

009                                               A BSTRACT
010
011
               Standard multi-agent reinforcement learning (MARL) algorithms are vulnera-
012
               ble to sim-to-real gaps. To address this, distributionally robust Markov games
013
               (RMGs) have been proposed to enhance robustness in MARL by optimizing the
014            worst-case performance when game dynamics shift within a prescribed uncer-
015            tainty set. Solving RMGs remains under-explored, from problem formulation to
016            the development of sample-efficient algorithms. A notorious yet open challenge is
017            if RMGs can escape the curse of multiagency, where the sample complexity scales
018            exponentially with the number of agents. In this work, we propose a natural class
019            of RMGs where the uncertainty set of each agent is shaped by both the environ-
020            ment and other agentsвАЩ strategies in a best-response manner. We first establish
021            the well-posedness of these RMGs by proving the existence of game-theoretic
022
               solutions such as robust Nash equilibria and coarse correlated equilibria (CCE).
               Assuming access to a generative model, we then introduce a sample-efficient al-
023
               gorithm for learning the CCE whose sample complexity scales polynomially with
024
               all relevant parameters. To the best of our knowledge, this is the first algorithm to
025            break the curse of multiagency for RMGs.
026
027
028
029   1   I NTRODUCTION
030
031   A flurry of problems naturally involve decision-making among multiple players with strategic objec-
032   tives. Multi-agent reinforcement learning (MARL) serves as a powerful framework to address these
033   challenges, demonstrating potential in various applications such as social dilemmas (Leibo et al.,
034   2017; Baker, 2020; Zhang et al., 2024), autonomous driving (Lillicrap et al., 2015), robotics (Kober
035   et al., 2013; Rusu et al., 2017), and games (Mnih et al., 2015; Vinyals et al., 2019). Despite the
036   recent success of standard MARL, its transition from prototypes to reliable production is hindered
037   by robustness concerns due to the complexity and variability of both the real-world environment
038   and human behaviors. Specifically, environmental uncertainty can arise from sim-to-real gaps (To-
039
      bin et al., 2017), unexpected disturbance (Pinto et al., 2017), system noise, and adversarial attacks
      (Mahmood et al., 2018); agentsвАЩ behaviors are subject to unknown bounded rationality and variabil-
040
      ity (Tversky & Kahneman, 1974). The solution learned at training time can fail catastrophically
041
      when faced with a slightly shifted MARL problem during testing, resulting in a significant drop in
042   overall outcomes and each agentвАЩs individual payoff (Balaji et al., 2019; Zhang et al., 2020a; Zeng
043   et al., 2022; Yeh et al., 2021; Shi et al., 2024; Slumbers et al., 2023).
044
045
      To address robustness challenges, a promising framework is (distributionally) robust Markov games
      (RMGs) (Littman, 1994; Shapley, 1953). It is a robust counterpart to the common playground of
046
      standard MARL problems вАФ Markov games (MGs) (Zhang et al., 2020c; KardesћІ et al., 2011). In
047
      standard MGs, agents consider (competitive) personal objectives and simultaneously interact with
048   each other within a shared unknown environment. The goal is to learn some solution concepts
049   called equilibria, which are joint strategies/policies of agents that all of them stick with rationally
050   with other agents fixed; for instance, Nash equilibria (NE) (Nash, 1951; Shapley, 1953), correlated
051   equilibria (CE), and coarse correlated equilibria (CCE) (Aumann, 1987; Moulin & Vial, 1978). To
052   promote robustness, RMGs differ from standard MGs by defining each agentвАЩs payoff (objective) as
053   its worst-case performance when the dynamics of the game shift within a prescribed uncertainty set
      centered around a nominal environment.


                                                        1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
      1.1    T HE CURSE OF MULTIAGENCY IN ROBUST MARL
055
056   Sample efficiency is a crucial metric for MARL due to the limited availability of data relative to the
057   high dimensionality of the problem. In MARL, agents strive to learn a rationally optimal solution
058   (equilibrium) through interactions with an unknown environment (Silver et al., 2016; Vinyals et al.,
059   2019; Achiam et al., 2023). In contemporary applications, the environment is often extremely large-
060
      scale, while data acquisition can be prohibitively limited by high costs and stakes. As such, a
      notable challenge in terms of scalability for sample efficiency in MARL is known as the curse of
061
      multiagency вАФ the sample complexity requirement scales exponentially with the number of agents
062
      (induced by the exponentially growing size of the joint action space). This issue has been recognized
063   and studied in extensive MARL problems, but remains open for robust MARL. We concentrate on
064   learning finite-horizon multi-player general-sum Markov games with a generative model (Kearns &
065   Singh, 1999), where the number of agents is n, the episode length is H, the size of the state space is
066   S, and the size of the i-th agentвАЩs action space is Ai , for 1 вЙ§ i вЙ§ n.
067
         вАҐ Breaking the curse of multiagency in standard MARL. A line of pioneering work (Jin et al.,
068
      2021; Bai & Jin, 2020; Song et al., 2021; Li et al., 2023) has recently introduced a new suite of
069   algorithms using adaptive sampling that provably break the curse of multiagency in standard MGs.
070   In particular, to find an ќµ-approximate CCE, Li et al. (2023) requires a sample complexity no more
071   than                                        4 Pn
072                                           e H S i=1 Ai
                                              O                                                      (1)
073                                                     ќµ2
074
      up to logarithmic factors, which depends only on the sum of individual actions, rather than the
075
      number of joint actions.
076
077       вАҐ The persistent curse of multiagency in robust MARL. The development of provable sample-
078   efficient algorithms for RMGs is largely underexplored, with only a few recent studies (Zhang et al.,
079   2020c; KardesћІ et al., 2011; Ma et al., 2023; Blanchet et al., 2023; Shi et al., 2024). Focusing on a
080
      class of RMGs with uncertainty sets satisfying the (s, a)-rectangularity condition, existing works all
      suffer from the curse of multiagency, significantly limiting their scalability. For example, using the
081
      total variation (TV) distance as the divergence function, the state-of-the-art (Shi et al., 2024), using
082
      non-adaptive sampling, finds an ќµ-approximate robust CCE with a sample complexity no more than
083
                                     3 Qn                                     o
084                                   H S i=1 Ai          n           1
                                 O
                                 e                   min H,                                                (2)
085                                         ќµ2                  min1вЙ§iвЙ§n ѕГi
086
087   up to logarithmic factors, where ѕГi вИИ [0, 1) is the uncertainty level for the i-th agent. As a result,
088   the sample size requirement becomes prohibitive when the number of agents is large.
089   Consequently, there is a significant desire to explore paths that could break through the curse of
090   multiagency in RMGs, which is much more involved than its standard counterpart due to compli-
091   cated non-linearity introduced by planning for worst-case performances. Nevertheless, the family of
092   RMGs is a much richer class of problems because of the flexibility in choosing the uncertainty sets
093   to capture different robust design considerations. While convenient, the (s, a)-rectangularity condi-
094   tion prevalent in current approaches can be overly restricted in practice, as each agentвАЩs uncertainty
095
      set is assumed to be independent of other agentsвАЩ strategies and can be decoupled into independent
      subsets for each state-joint action pair (s, a), suggesting it might be challenging to break the curse
096
      of multiagency in the existing framework. Given these limitations, we are motivated to develop new
097
      classes of RMGs that can provide robust solutions applicable to more realistic MARL problems with
098   sample-efficient algorithms. This raises an open question:
099
100         Can we design RMGs with practically-meaningful uncertainty sets that come with sample
101                       complexity guarantees breaking the curse of multiagency?
102
      1.2    C ONTRIBUTIONS
103
104   We propose a new class of RMGs with a fictitious uncertainty set that explicitly captures uncertain-
105   ties in the environment in view of other agentsвАЩ strategies, making it suitable for complex real-world
106   scenarios. We begin by verifying the game-theoretic properties of the proposed class of RMGs to
107   ensure the existence of robust variants of well-known standard equilibria notions, robust NE and ro-
      bust CCE. Next, due to the general intractability of learning NE, we focus on designing algorithms

125      вАҐ We introduce a new class of robust Markov games using fictitious uncertainty sets with policy-
126   induced (s, ai )-rectangularity condition (see Section 2.2 for details), which is a natural adaptation
127   from robust single-agent RL to robust MARL. The uncertainty set for each agent i can be decom-
128   posed into independent subsets over each state and its own action tuple (s, ai ), where each subset is
129   a вАЬballвАЭ around the expected nominal transition determined by other agentsвАЩ policies and the nomi-
130
      nal transition kernel, a divergence function ѕБ, and the radius/uncertainty level ѕГi . We verify several
      essential facts of this class of RMGs: the existence of the desired equilibrium вАФ robust NE and
131
      robust CCE for this new class of RMGs using game-theoretical tools such as fixed-point theorem;
132
      the existence of best-response policies and robust Bellman equations.
133

      that can find ќµ-approximate robust CCE with high probability, as long as the sample size exceeds

159
160   In this section, we begin with some background on multi-agent general-sum standard Markov games
161   (MGs) in finite-horizon settings, followed by a general framework of a robust variant of standard
      MGs вАФ- distributionally robust Markov games.

195   2.2   D ISTRIBUTIONALLY ROBUST M ARKOV GAMES
196
197   A general distributionally robust Markov game (RMG) is represented by the tuple
198                           RMG = S, {Ai }1вЙ§iвЙ§n , {UѕБѕГi (P 0 )}1вЙ§iвЙ§n , r, H .

199
200   Here, S, {Ai }1вЙ§iвЙ§n , r, H are defined in the same manner as those in standard MGs (see Sec-
201   tion 2.1). RMGs differ from standard MGs: for each agent i (1 вЙ§ i вЙ§ n), the transition kernel is not
202
      fixed but can vary within its own prescribed uncertainty set UѕБѕГi (P 0 ) centered around some nominal
203   kernel P 0 : S √ЧA 7вЖТ вИЖ(S) that represents a reference (such as the training environment). The shape
204
      and the size of the uncertainty set {UѕБѕГi (P 0 )}iвИИ[n] are further specified by a divergence function ѕБ
205   and the uncertainty levels {ѕГi }iвИИ[n] , serving as the вАЬdistanceвАЭ metric and the radius respectively.
206   Various choices of the divergence function have been considered in robust RL literature, including
207   but not limited to f -divergence (such as total variation, ѕЗ2 divergence, and Kullback-Leibler (KL)
208   divergence) (Yang et al., 2022; Zhou et al., 2021; Lu et al., 2024; Wang et al., 2024) and Wasserstein
209   distance (Xu et al., 2023). Different uncertainty sets lead to distinct RMGs, as they address distinct
210   types of uncertainty and game-theoretical solutions. This paper focuses on variability in environ-
211   mental dynamics (transition kernels), though uncertainty in agentsвАЩ reward functions could also be
212
      considered similarly but is omitted for brevity.
213   Robust value functions and best-response policies. For any RMG, each agent seeks to maximize
214   its worst-case performance in the presence of other agentsвАЩ behaviors despite perturbations in the
215   environment dynamics, as long as the kernel transitions remain within its prescribed uncertainty set
      UѕБѕГi (P 0 ). Mathematically, given any joint policy ѕА : S √Ч [H] 7вЖТ вИЖ(A), the worst-case performance

255   This correlated policy ѕА CCE can also be viewed as a distribution ќЊ over the product policy space since
256   each joint action a can be seen as a deterministic product policy. Careful readers may note that the
257   definition (9) of CCE in standard MGs is in a different form from the one (8) in RMGs, as the latter
258   does not include the expectation operator EѕАвИЉќЊ [¬Ј] with respect to the policy distribution (ќЊ) over the
259   value function. We emphasize that the definition with the expectation operator outside of the value
260   (or cost) function with respect to a distribution of product pure strategies in (8) is a natural formula-
261   tion originating from game theory (Moulin et al., 2014; Moulin & Vial, 1978). In standard MARL
262
      and previous robust MARL studies, the definition in (9) is typically used because (9) and (8) are iden-
      tical in those situations, as the expectation operator and the corresponding value functions are linear
263
      with respect to the joint policy, allowing them to be interchanged (Li et al., 2023; Shi et al., 2024).

266   3     ROBUST M ARKOV G AMES WITH F ICTITIOUS U NCERTAINTY S ETS
267
268   Given the definition of general RMGs, a natural question arises: what kinds of uncertainty sets
269   should we consider to achieve the desired robustness in our solutions? To address this, we focus on
      a specific class of RMGs characterized by a type of natural yet powerful uncertainty sets.


                                                                                 5
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


270
      3.1   A NOVEL UNCERTAINTY SET DEFINITION IN RMG S
271
272   We propose a new class of uncertainty sets, named fictitious uncertainty sets, which count in the
273   uncertainty induced by both the environment and agentsвАЩ behaviors in a correlated manner. Before

299                             ѕАвИТi

302   Further discussions of fictitious uncertainty sets. It is in order to remark on the proposed type of
303   uncertainty sets, in comparison with prior works.
304
         вАҐ A natural adaptation from single-agent robust RL. When agents follow some joint policy ѕА :
305
      S √Ч [H] 7вЖТ вИЖ(A), fixing other agentsвАЩ policy ѕАвИТi , from the perspective of each individual agent
306   i, RMGs with our policy-induced (s, ai )-rectangularity condition will degrade to a single-agent
307   robust RL problem with the widely used (s, ai )-rectangularity condition in the single-agent literature
308   (Iyengar, 2005; Zhou et al., 2021). Namely, from any agent iвАЩs viewpoint, in a RMG, it has an
309   вАЭoverall environmentвАЭ player that can not only manipulate the environmental dynamics but also
310   other playersвАЩ policy ѕАвИТi .
311
         вАҐ Allowing uncertainty from both the environment and agentsвАЩ behaviors in a correlated manner.
312   One essential feature of our proposed uncertainty set is that it is shaped by both the environment and
313   agentsвАЩ strategies in a (possibly)         manner. Specifically, for any agent i and a given policy ѕА,
                                      correlated

314                              ѕГi    ѕАвИТi
      any uncertainty subset UѕБ Ph,s,ai (over any (h, s, ai )) is constructed as a neighborhood around
315                        ѕА
316
                            вИТi
      a nominal center Ph,s,a  i
                                 (see (10)) that depends on both the nominal environment P 0 and other
317   agentsвАЩ conditional strategies ѕАh (¬Ј | s, ai ).
318      вАҐ Comparisons to prior works. Prior works on provable sample-efficient algorithms have fo-
319   cused on a different type of uncertainty sets with (s, a)-rectangularity condition (Ma et al., 2023;
320   Blanchet et al., 2023; Shi et al., 2024). This class of uncertainty sets decouples the uncertainty into
321   independent subsets for each state-joint action pair (s, a), accounting for the uncertainty of the envi-
322   ronment and agentsвАЩ strategies independently. In comparison, the proposed uncertainty set lifts this
323   independence assumption across subsets over different (s, ai , aвИТi ) for any aвИТi вИИ Ai , enabling the
      environment and agentsвАЩ strategies to shape the uncertainty set in a correlated manner.

353   4     S AMPLE -E FFICIENT L EARNING : A LGORITHM AND T HEORY
354
355
      In this section, we focus on designing sample-efficient algorithms for solving fictitious RMGs when
356   agents need to collect data by interacting with the unknown shared environment in order to learn
357   the equilibria. To proceed, we shall first specify the data collection mechanism and the divergence
358   function for the uncertainty set. Then we propose a sample-efficient algorithm Robust-Q-FTRL
359   that leverages a carefully-designed adaptive sampling strategy to break the curse of multiagency.
360
361
      4.1   P ROBLEM SETTING AND GOAL

363   certainty level to control its shape and size. In this work, we focus on using the TV distance as the
364   divergence function ѕБ for the uncertainty set, following Szita et al. (2003); Lee et al. (2021); Pan
365   et al. (2023), defined by

384
      4.2    A LGORITHM DESIGN
385
386   With the sampling mechanism over a generative model in hand, we propose an algorithm called
387   Robust-Q-FTRL to learn an ќµ-robust CCE in a sample-efficient manner, summarized in Algorithm 2
388   in the appendix. Robust-Q-FTRL draws inspiration from Q-FTRL developed in the standard MG
389   literature (Li et al., 2022), but empowers tailored designs for learning in fictitious RMGs to achieve
390   a robust equilibrium and to tackle statistical challenges arising from agentsвАЩ nonlinear objectives.
391
      Overall, Robust-Q-FTRL takes a single pass to learn recursively from the final time step h = H to
      h = 1. At each time step h вИИ [H], an online learning process with K iterations will be executed.
392
      Before introducing the algorithm, we first concentrate on two essential steps customized for learning
393
      in fictitious RMGs.
394

      the implications of our sample complexity results.
500   Breaking the curse of multiagency in the sample complexity for RMGs. Theorem 2 demonstrates
501   that for any fictitious RMGs, Robust-Q-FTRL algorithm finds an ѕµ-robust CCE when the total
502   number of samples exceeds

506   To the best of our knowledge, Robust-Q-FTRL with the above sample complexity in (19) is the first
507   algorithm for RMGs breaking the curse of multiagency, regardless of the typesPof uncertainty sets.
                                                                                     n
508   Our sample complexity
                     Qn          depends linearly on the sum of each agentвАЩs actions i=1 Ai rather than
509   their product i=1 Ai вАФmaking the algorithm highly scalable as the number of agents increases.
510   Nonetheless, there still exist gaps between our upper bound and the lower boundвАФespecially in
511   terms o the dependency on the horizon length H and the accuracy level ќµвАФan interesting direction
512   to investigate in the future.
513   Comparisons with prior works. All prior works focus on learning equilibria for a different kind of
514   robust MGs with (s, a)-rectangular uncertainty sets (Ma et al.,
                                                                    2023;   Blanchet etnal., 2023; Shi et o
                                                                                                          al.,
                                                                      SH 3 n

      2024). However, the state-of-the-art sample complexity O  e          2
                                                                           ќµ      min H,      min1вЙ§iвЙ§n ѕГi
516
      (Shi et al., 2024) still suffers from the curse of multiagency with an exponential dependency on the
517
      number of agents when all agents have equal action spaces, which uses nonadaptive sampling. Our
518   work circumvents the curse of multiagency by resorting to a tailored adaptive sampling and online
519   learning procedure, together with the introduction of a new class of fictitious RMGs, providing a
520   fresh perspective to learning RMGs.
521
      Technical insights. For sample complexity analysis, while previous works have addressed the curse
522
      of multiagency in sequential games like standard Markov games (MGs) and Markov potential games,
523
      these methods are not directly applicable to RMGs. Prior approaches assume a linear relationship
524   between the value function and the transition kernel, allowing statistical errors across K iterations to
525   cancel out. However, in RMGs, the robust value function, due to its distributionally robust require-
526   ment, is highly nonlinear and often lacks a closed form, making it impossible to linearly aggregate
527   statistical errors. To tackle the nonlinear challenges in RMGs, we design a variance-style bonus term
528   through non-trivial decomposition and control of auxiliary statistical errors caused by nonlinearity,
529   resulting in a tight upper bound on regret during the online learning process.
530
531   5   C ONCLUSION
532
533
      Robustness in MARL presents greater challenges than in single-agent RL due to the strategic inter-
534   actions between agents in a game-theoretic setting. This work proposes a new class of RMGs with
535   fictitious uncertainty sets that naturally extends from robust single-agent RL and addresses more
536   realistic scenarios where each agentвАЩs uncertainty is influenced by both the environment and the be-
537   havior of others. We then propose Robust-Q-FTRL, the first algorithm to break the curse of multia-
538   gency in robust Markov games regardless of the uncertainty set definitions, with sample complexity
539   scaling polynomially with all key parameters. This opens up new research directions in MARL,
      such as uncertainty set selection, equilibrium refinement, and sample-efficient algorithm design.

807        A    R ELATED WORK
808
809        Breaking curse of multiagency for standard Markov games. Breaking the curse of multiagency
           is a major and prevalent challenge in sequential games. In standard multi-agent general-sum MGs,


                                                                    15
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


810
      it has been shown that learning a Nash equilibrium requires an exponential sample complexity
811   (Song et al., 2021; Rubinstein, 2017; Bai & Jin, 2020). However, for other types of equilibria,
812   such as CE and CCE, many works have successfully broken the curse of multiagency. Specifi-
813   cally, for finite-horizon general-sum MGs in the tabular setting with finite state and action spaces,
814   Jin et al. (2021) developed the V-learning algorithm for learning CE and CCE with the sample
815   complexity of O(H e 6 S(maxiвИИ[n] Ai )2 /ѕµ2 ) and O(H
                                                       e 6 S maxiвИИ[n] Ai /ѕµ2 ), respectively; Daskalakis
816   et al. (2023) achieved a sample complexity of O(H e 11 S 3 maxiвИИ[n] Ai /ѕµ3 ) for learning a CCE.
817   Beyond tabular settings, Wang et al. (2023) and Cui et al. (2023) extended these results
818
                                                                                                   to lin-
      ear function approximation, achieving sample complexities of O(d  e 4 H 6 maxiвИИ[n] A5 /ѕµ2 ) and
                                                                                               i
819
       e 10 d4 log maxiвИИ[n] Ai /ѕµ4 ), respectively, where d is the dimension of the linear features. For
      O(H
820
      Markov potential games, a subclass of MGs, Song et al. (2021) provided a centralized algorithm that
821                                              e 4 S 2 maxiвИИ[n] Ai /ѕµ3 ).
      learns a NE with a sample complexity of O(H
822
823   Finite-sample analysis for distributionally robust Markov games. Robust Markov games under
824   environmental uncertainty are largely underexplored, with only a few provable algorithms (Zhang
825   et al., 2020a; KardesћІ et al., 2011; Ma et al., 2023; Blanchet et al., 2023; Shi et al., 2024). Exist-
826   ing sample complexity analyses all suffer from the daunting curse of multiagency issues, or impose
827
      an extremely restricted uncertainty level that can fail to deliver the desired robustness (Ma et al.,
      2023; Blanchet et al., 2024; Shi et al., 2024). Specifically, they all consider a class of RMGs with
828
      the (s, a)-rectangularity condition, where the uncertainty sets for each agent can be decomposed
829   into independent sets over each (s, a) pair. Shi et al. (2024) considered the generative model with
830   an uncertainty set measured by the TV distance, Blanchet et al. (2023) treated a different sam-
831   pling mechanism with offline data for both the TV distance and KL divergence. In addition, Ma
832   et al. (2023) required the uncertainty level be much smaller than the accuracy-level and an instance-
                                                ќµ     pmin
833   dependent parameter (i.e., ѕГi вЙ§ max{ SH     2 , H } for all i вИИ [n]). This can thus fail to maintain the

834   desired robustness, especially when the accuracy requirement is high (i.e., ќµ вЖТ 0) or the RMG has
835   small minimal positive transition probabilities (i.e., pmin вЖТ 0).
836   Robust MARL. Standard MARL algorithms may overfit the training environment and could fail
837   dramatically due to the perturbations and variability of both agentsвАЩ behaviors and the shared envi-
838   ronment, leading to performance drop and large deviation from the equilibrium. To address this, this
839   work considers a robust variant of MARL adopting the distributionally robust optimization (DRO)
840   framework that has primarily been investigated in supervised learning (Rahimian & Mehrotra, 2019;
841   Gao, 2020; Bertsimas et al., 2018; Duchi & Namkoong, 2018; Blanchet & Murthy, 2019) and has
842   attracted a lot of attention in promoting robustness in single-agent RL (Nilim & El Ghaoui, 2005;
843   Iyengar, 2005; Badrinath & Kalathil, 2021; Zhou et al., 2021; Shi & Chi, 2024; Wang et al., 2024;
844
      Shi et al., 2023). Beyond the RMG framework considered in this work, recent research has advanced
      the robustness of MARL algorithms from various perspectives, including resilience to uncertainties
845
      or attacks on states (Han et al., 2022; Zhou & Liu, 2023), the type of agents (Zhang et al., 2021),
846
      other agentsвАЩ policies (Li et al., 2019; Kannan et al., 2023), offline data poisoning (Wu et al., 2024;
847   McMahan et al., 2024), and nonstationary environment (Szita et al., 2003). A recent review can be
848   found in Vial et al. (2022).

854   Denoting the vectors x = [xi ]1вЙ§iвЙ§n and y = [yi ]1вЙ§iвЙ§n , we use the notation x вЙ§ y (or x вЙ• y) to
855   signify that xi вЙ§ yi (or xi вЙ• yi )  for every 1  вЙ§ i вЙ§ n. The Hadamard product of two vectors x
856   and y in RS is denoted as x вЧ¶ y = x(s) ¬Ј y(s) sвИИS . In addition, for any series of vectors {xi }iвИИ[S ,
857   diag(x1 , x2 , ¬Ј ¬Ј ¬Ј , xS ) denote a block diagonal matrix by placing each given vector xi along the
858   diagonal, with zeros filling the off-diagonal blocks. 0 (or 1) represents the all-zero (or all-one)
859   vector, while ei вИИ RS denotes a basis vector of dimension S with 1 in the i-th position and 0
860
      elsewhere.

968   B.3     P RELIMINARY FACTS ABOUT FTRL
969
970   Our proposed algorithm (see Algorithm 2) is inspired by online adversarial learning. In this section,
971   we introduce the formulation of online learning and review key aspects of a widely-used algorithm,
      the Follow-the-Regularized-Leader (FTRL) algorithm.


                                                                    18
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


972
       Problem setting: online learning for weighted average loss. We consider an online learning prob-
973    lem over K steps, commonly found in adversarial learning settings (Lattimore & SzepesvaћБri, 2020).
974    The learner is presented with an action set A, and loss functions f1 , . . . , fK : A вЖТ RвЙ•0 are provided
975    for each step. At each time step k, the learner selects a distribution over the action set, ѕАk вИИ вИЖ(A),
976    and observes the loss function fk (ѕАk ). The goal of the learner is to minimize the weighted aver-
                                                                 PK
977    age loss over the K steps, which is defined as: LK = k=1 ќ±kK fk (ѕАk ). To evaluate the learnerвАЩs
978    performance, the regret for the online learning process is defined as:

                                         K                            K

                                              k=1                            k=1

984    described above is the Follow-the-Regularized-Leader (FTRL) algorithm, introduced by Shalev-
985    Shwartz & Singer (2007); Shalev-Shwartz (2007). At each step k + 1, the learner selects a soft-
986    greedy action by solving:

1022   Definition 2 (Upper semi-continuous). A point-to-set mapping x вИИ X 7вЖТ ѕХ(x) вИИ Y is upper
1023
       semi-continuous if limnвЖТвИЮ xn = x0 , y n вИИ ѕХ(xn ), limnвЖТвИЮ y n = y0 imply that y 0 вИИ ѕХ(x0 ).
1024   Theorem 4 (KakutaniвАЩs fixed point Theorem (Kakutani, 1941)). If X is a closed, bounded, and
1025   convex set in a Euclidean space, and ѕХ is a upper semi-continuous correspondence mapping X into
       the family of all closed convex subsets of X, then there exists x вИИ X so that x вИИ ѕХ(x).
